0345803485|5.0|I have to confess that I wasn't necessarily in love with this book from the get-go.  My initial perceptions of the enigmatic Christian Grey were not immensely favorable, simply because I couldn't get behind the S&M portions of the book (no pun intended).  However, the character and the book are simply not that one-dimensional to be broad-brushed as an S&M fantasy novel.  The more you read, the more you realize that this is in fact a fantastic story of the evolution and redemption of a tragically dark man who winds up shining through the love of the female lead, Anastasia Steele. It's a brilliant love story. It's a meeting of minds (and certainly bodies) in an emotional roller coaster ride that you simply don't want to end. And fortunately, it doesn't. Book two is, in my opinion, many times better than the first.  It's also worth noting that there are also grin-inducing light moments in the book - unquestionably, my favorite passages are the humorously and artfully written emails betweeen Christian and Ana.<br /><br />That said, I realize this novel has received more than a fair amount of flack for its technical flaws. As an author, I can relate to the highly positive and negative reviews; I certainly do not wish to draw the ire of those passionately set against the merits of this book, but with all due respect, most people condemning the technical flaws have never written a novel, much less a trilogy. Granted, blatant flaws probably should have been edited out (over-used words and phrases, oddly placed language, British vernacular, etc.), but in the end, it doesn't change the fact that this woman told a wonderful love story. In any book, there is certainly a measure of technical prowess, but equally important if not moreso is the ability to really speak to the audience and potentially profoundly affect them. There is no question that this book has done just that. The numbers speak for themselves. Millions of women can't be wrong. E.L. James has successfully taken a taboo genre, brought it to the mainstream, and left us begging for more.  Kudos on a job well done!<br /><br />-J.L. B004QOB8SO|5.0|While Season One of BBC's SHERLOCK was insanely entertaining, incredibly intelligent, and remarkably well-acted, just three episodes wasn't enough. Steven Moffat and Mark Gatiss did an extraordinary thing with the iconic tales of Sir Arthur Conan Doyle: They MODERNIZED it. While that isn't a particularly remarkable thing in and of itself, what REALLY is extraordinary about this series is that the modernization WORKED flawlessly.<br /><br />This season (which just ended last night in the U.S. being broadcast on PBS with some rather controversial editing since apparently eight minutes of each episode had to be chopped off to make room for PBS advertising) was not just better than the first season, it was more involving from a character standpoint. We got a much better glimpse into the souls of Sherlock Holmes (the wonderful Benedict Cumberbatch, who is rapidly becoming one of the hardest working men in Hollywood as well) and his faithful companion Dr. John Watson (the equally great Martin Freeman). The wonderful thing that this show has done since its inception has been to not marginalize the friendship between Holmes and Watson, but also not marginalizing Watson himself. While he is in constant awe of Holmes' remarkable deductive skills, he's also his own man and not the plump, blundering buffoonish character of old adaptations, and he makes important contributions to each case.<br /><br />This season does only give us three more episodes, but each episode is essentially one feature-length film. The first episode is the best of the entire series yet, \\"A Scandal In Belgravia\\", which introduces this incarnation of Sherlock Holmes to the character of Irene Adler, who casual fans of the detective have seen as portrayed by Rachel McAdams in both of the Downey Jr./Law SHERLOCK HOLMES films. She is originally, as portrayed in Guy Ritchie's entertaining films, an American who moves to Europe and becomes a \\"courtesan\\" who is something of a foil and love interest for Holmes. In this episode though, Adler (Lara Pulver of MI-5) is a dominatrix who has enough secrets on her mobile phone to practically bring down the entirety of England. She enters into something of a game of obsession with Holmes. This game does bring other players, naturally, and soon Holmes is looking at something much larger. Cumberbatch and Pulver bring a very heightened sense of sexual tension to their scenes together, as Holmes feels something more for this woman than for any other woman in his life. Although, I have to say that arguably my favorite part in this episode is where we see how strong the bonds are between Holmes and his maternal landlady Mrs. Hudson (the wonderful Una Stubbs). The message is clear: Don't mess with the friends of Sherlock Holmes.<br /><br />Then comes \\"The Hounds of Baskerville\\", which is the least of this season. A troubled young man comes to Holmes and Watson with the tale of a gigantic hound that killed his father years before in the countryside area of Dartmoor. At first, Holmes is astonishingly bored with the idea of this case, but something the young man says specifically pricks up his ears and the game is afoot. Also in Dartmoor is a government research facility called Baskerville, where apparently many secret experiments are being done, including genetic research. Could there be a military experiment of the creation of a huge dog? Is the explanation more complex even than that? There were many things to love about this episode, with one of my favorite moments being that of Holmes trying to quit smoking and references a \\"seven percent solution\\", but this was the only episode when I felt like I was ahead of Holmes and exactly what was happening and how it was happening. It still works and it is still very entertaining, particularly Cumberbatch's performance as he first truly feels the effects of fear and panic for perhaps the first time in his life.<br /><br />Finally, we have \\"The Reichenbach Fall\\", which is a reference to the finalconfrontation between Holmes and his arch-nemesis James Moriarty. This episode, like the finale of the first season \\"The Great Game\\", is extremely dark and foreboding, as well it should be since you can't really have a light-hearted take on the mental combat between Holmes and Moriarty (played with flamboyant and psychotic relish by Andrew Scott). There's nothing I really want to say about this episode that can do it justice, but it's incredibly smart, incredibly tense and incredibly disturbing.<br /><br />The other thing that the show has been so brilliant at doing is giving a more realistic (and more modern) take on the friendship between Holmes and Watson. It never tries to imply that they are more than friends, but the hordes of readers and critics of the Holmes/Watson partnership over the decades have done enough examinations of their own to come to the illogical conclusion that Holmes and Watson are lovers. These people are repeatedly poked fun at via the voice of Watson as he reads or hears or sees people who see the two of them as lovers, which is a bit of a running gag throughout the season, and Freeman plays it perfectly with just the right mixture of offense and incredulity. The other primary relationship here is that of Sherlock's relationship with his brother Mycroft (played by Mark Gatiss). Their relationship is strained beyond repair, but there's something touching about Mycroft's concern for his younger brother while at the same time, as in \\"A Scandal in Belgravia\\", where Mycroft knows his brother's incapability to work within the confines of normal society.<br /><br />It's always a joy to see a program about extremely intelligent people that is created by extremely intelligent people. While I've never been a fan of DOCTOR WHO, I was extremely impressed with Moffat's updating of the Jekyll/Hyde tale in the series JEKYLL with the great James Nesbitt.<br /><br />SHERLOCK will have \\"competition\\" soon on U.S. television as the upcoming CBS series ELEMENTARY with Johnny Lee Miller as Holmes and Lucy Liu as Watson (?) in yet another update, but apparently with the added attraction of doing some gender-bending. If this is anything like all the other procedural mysteries on CBS, it will probably be hugely successful from a ratings standpoint, but will be a massive creative failure. But it might well get people interested in this series, and that might just be good enough.confrontation between Holmes and his arch-nemesis James Moriarty. 0307346617|4.0|I'm a fan of zombie movies, or to be more specific I'm a fan of the first HALF of zombie movies. The first thirty or so minutes of a zombie flick when the world goes from as constructed & settled to mad brain chomping, intestine ripping, fending off your now ravenous husband & children with a rusty shovel absolute pandemonium are the most exhilarating fun I'm capable of having at the theatre. Eventually though, the person through whom we are witnessing the undead apocalypse escapes, meets up with other survivors and they inevitably decide to hole up in a house, a military base, a mall etc until help arrives. Sometimes it does, usually it doesn't but by that time the tension has dissipated and I'm too bored and uninvested to really care. This problem has plagued all the zombie movies I've seen, even the really good ones. The Walking Dead is an excellent comic & program that looks to explore the dynamics of one such group by fleshing out the typical zombie flick archetypes with more attention to character and detail, but as good as it is, the truth is that even well written characters get boring when all they're left to do is wax philosophical about the end of the world until the next encounter with the undead.<br /><br />I was pleased to see that World War Z avoided (most) of these pitfalls. Written by Max Brooks, author of 'The Zombie Survival Guide' and something of a zombie fanatic, World War Z (guess what the Z is for) doesn't attempt to rewrite the typical zombie narrative but instead of focusing on the trials and terrors particular to one group of survivors (usually North American,) Brooks writes from the perspective of a whole array of people of differing races, nationalities & religions from all across the globe. From rural China (the origin of this book universe's undead outbreak) to India, Israel, The United Kingdom, The Andes Mountains of Chile, Russia, Japan, under water and even from Outer Space. The book itself is semi-Meta, written in the form of a book that seeks to interview a variety of individuals from all different walks of life & document their experiences in the now (mostly) finished zombie war. CIA Agents, Israeli spies, Mongolian smugglers, Russian soldiers, Japanese cyberpunks, Air Force pilots and lots of plain garden variety citizens each take turns telling their stories from their particular perspectives and vantage points, all during different stages of the global zombie outbreak. The result is you aren't bogged down and potentially bored with the perspective of just one person or group, but are essentially viewing (err reading) 11 or 12 different zombies. The ability to concentrate on just the interesting parts keeps the narrative fresh and all the varied narratives from different sections of society & the world creates a patchwork effect that allows the reader to see the Zombie Outbreak not just on a micro but on a Macro scale I.E. The decisions world leaders end up making when confronted with the dead returning to life, how it affects their citizens and how it affects their relations with other countries.<br /><br />The book is not without flaws (you're kidding!). Brooks has to inhabit a number of different characters from different countries & differing personalities and paint their experiences as believable and he mostly succeeds in this regard. Writing from the perspective of someone from a different country and socio-economic background, who speaks a different language, practices a different religion and is sometimes a different gender isn't easy. While some of the characters are quite lovingly & meticulously portrayed others are unfortunately little better than cliches & sterotypes, the British and many of the Russian characters in particular were particularly corny & eye-roll inducing. I was also a little bit let down that he didn't put more focus on the international level and the ways in which the zombie outbreak would impact International relations, both between allies and adversaries, which is something the first half of the book briefly delves into, but doesn't really follow through with on the last half.<br /><br />This is the written equivalent of an action movie, though one artfully executred so be prepared for a fair share of cornball moments, but in general a good read.

B007MAETA8|3.0|Being as fair as I can be for someone who has been to eight concerts and owns pretty much every piece of his released work and more, I would have to say that this is not John's best work. Before anyone gets into the hissyfit about \\"growing as an artist\\", let me stop you there and say that I completely agree that no artist should ever stay stagnant and exploration is truly the best way one can express one's creativity. That being said, I don't know how to really feel about the trajectory of these past two albums.<br /><br />If we follow the course charted out by his earliest work, Room for Squares, we see that even through the folky adolescent angst of a young man's lyrics, we hear the underpinnings of an artist setting himself to wade in the deep waters of a Blues Revival movement (i.e. City Love and Neon). In interview after interview he had always talked about making Blues music more accessible. With Heavier Things, he continued to push this agenda forward with songs like Come Back to Bed and to a degree, Daughters. At this point, if you're lucky enough to attend the live shows, the long Bluesy solos should clue you into what direction he's gravitating towards.<br /><br />To solidify this argument I present to you, The John Mayer Trio. All out, no apologies, I don't give a funk BLUES! Truly an amazing album and concert series. Many of the younger fans ignored it, but for those of us waiting for what seemed like a grand inevitability, it was quite the epiphany. Armed with the fuel of this experimental album, he went back to the studio and gave what many consider his greatest work, Continuum. With this album, I believe he achieved what he set out to do all along; producing pop-centered tunes with a ooey gooey Bluesy center. To me, it was the pinnacle of his music career.<br /><br />So, deciding that he wouldn't try to top that, he chose to change directions in the form of Battle Studies, an album that seemed lacking in the writing department and played too heavily to its retro California style pop theme. While it's hard to go wrong with John's great vocals, the album itself seemed to be mostly forgettable. There isn't really one song on the album that has ever stopped me in my tracks the way that 3x5 has, or tugged at my heart strings the way Slow Dancing in a Burning Room has.<br /><br />And now comes Born and Raised, which is an even deeper departure from those beloved Blues sensibilities. It's almost as if he's turned his back on it completely, and replaced it with a very lazy sounding album that, while trying to remain true to some semblance of a theme, muddies the beginning of one track with the end of another. After five or six listens, I don't know that any of these songs make me feel anything but indifference. Again, the voice is great. If you can't get enough of John's warm embracing vocals, you'll buy this album. It's hard not to like at least a few songs of any one album, and you could definitely leave it on in the background while you're at work or relaxing from a hard day at work. But is that really what we had hoped for from John, since the we were blessed with the energy and soulfulness of Continuum.<br /><br />Honestly, it feels like a betrayal of sorts. Many of us have followed him down a path that we believed would lead to something great. And it did! But then, at the height of all of this finely tuned writing, powerful singing and downright kickass guitar playing, he pulled the rug from under us and decided that he was done with all that. He's moved on and has left many of us wondering if maybe we should do the same.<br /><br />John's said different things throughout the years in terms of where he wanted his future albums to go. At one time, he said Continuum would be more of a classic R&B style, which I think it had some elements of, but not enough to considered truly R&B. How great would it be if he could take that Blues sentimentality and throw in some R&B style vocals. That's what I'm waiting for. Whatever Born and Raised is, it's commendable and it's palatable, but far from anything I would call great.<br /><br />UPDATE: After many months of repeated listening, I definitely have a greater appreciation of the album. It is definitely a mood setting album. You will either need to be in the mood for it, or it will put you in one. This is not to say that I am negating anything I had said earlier. There's a raw power and energy that I found in Continuum and John Mayer Trio that is still lacking here. But it's not so bad.

B007O3QC8K|3.0|This performance commands attention. It is superior to the Fourth which appeared a few years ago.  The Berlin Philharmonic play magnificently (and not least, the double-basses and cellos). The recording is good. Sir Simon is more adept than previously in this domain: the long lines are less of a trial. His patience is admirable.  One does not become a master Brucknerian overnight but he is showing progress (to wit, the fugue in the finale where Sir Simon builds up a tremendous head of steam - it's the best I have heard).  There is still cause for concern whenever the score is in `second gear' but this performance does not fail from 'the sag'. Above all, this is a noble enterprise. There is no excuse nowadays not to play the finale. Hopefully others will follow Sir Simon's lead.<br /><br />Even so, it is not the Word. Here is a quick critique of the first movement. I nitpick but Sir Simon and the Berliners warrant comparison with the best.<br /><br />At the opening, the exchange between the woodwind and brass is somewhat brusque. I don't detect any \\"emergence from the primordial fog\\" such as Barenboim encapsulates so well [[ASIN:B000000SDZ Bruckner: Symphony No. 9]].  Sir Simon is clearly not conversant with that memorable little tome \\"Anton Bruckner and the Art of the Infinitesimal Delay.\\"<br /><br />If there is any tension in the episode for woodwind at 11'41\\" to 12'34\\" , I cannot hear it. Likewise at 14'29\\"ff .<br /><br />The titanic outburst that occurs at 15'35\\" ends tamely. In the Barenboim alternative, Marcus Curtius - in the guise of the brass - rides his stallion into the abyss. Here, it is inaudible.<br /><br />It is  a weak handover at 21'10\\"<br /><br />The coda - sadly - is unsubtle. Rattle focuses so much on the first violins to the detriment of the wider aural picture.  He also shifts hastily from second gear to fourth if not fifth gear (22'43\\") and then consequently has nothing more to give at 23'11. Contrast this with the Barenboim who waits until the very last second beyond shifting into Fifth and memorably so (24'44\\").<br /><br />As above, these are relatively minor points. There is however, a cardinal weakness that prevents this performance of the Bruckner Ninth from disturbing the likes of Furtwangler (44), Herbie (1966 & 1976) and Barenboim (Berlin, not Chicago) with the latter being my preferred version.<br /><br />It is easy - in a way - to play the Bruckner Ninth as \\"Tod und Verkl&auml;rung with ostintatos\\". It ain't. There is a sizeable dose of psychosis and dread to it.  As so often, one defaults to Robert Simpson for clarity. \\"In the first movement of the Eighth the sense of tragedy is objectively expressed, the tonal conflicts of the Fifth Symphony suggest a great classical fresco, in the strange tonal tensions in the finale of the Sixth we do not feel as if the composer is himself terrified by his own fantasies. But in the Ninth we sometimes receive this impression, not so much in the ferocious scherzo as in parts of the first movement and large tracts of the tormented Adagio.\\"<br /><br />What a concept: of a composer being terrified by his own creation.<br /><br />I find no trace of this here. High drama - yes. Poetry - yes.  Anger - yes, but not mortal terror. Mystery is likewise in short supply.<br /><br />And quite frankly, the Finale is a disappointment and the blame does not lie with the performers in any way - they give their all. Wildner's edition is clearly superior.<br /><br />Every coda in the Bruckner canon is a standalone feature. I do not know of a single instance where it is welded to the previous orchestral tutti - but that's what we get here. My fellow reviewer Ralph was right to lament the absence of the bridging material in the build up to the coda. Momentum in Bruckner is not a linear concept - much like Mallory's assault on Everest, Bruckner stops, breathes and then resumes the ascent. Here, it is both muddled and madcapped with no such segmentation. Moreover, why quote the twothirds of the main theme from the first movement and not the remainder?<br /><br />In short, the scholars (Samale/Mazzuca/Phillips/Cohrs) have buggered up the end of the finale by over-ratiocination. Given that the movement is only five-sixths complete - and Bruckner surely would have revised a complete version before sending it off to the publisher - why not run with the creative inspiration that produced the earlier version? That includes the full repeat of the main theme from the first movement in all its glory - man, it works.<br /><br />Finale-wise, the Wildner is still indispensable [[ASIN:B0000BX5KD Bruckner: Symphony No. 9]]. Sure, the Berlin Phil are the better outfit but not so much that they blow away their country-cousins altogether. In fact, one could argue that the Berliners are surpassed by the Westphalians in the majestic reappearance of the chorale some sixteen minutes in. The latter are punchier.<br /><br />All in all, this is a three stars job.ds of the main theme from the first movement and not the remainder?<br /><br />In short, the scholars (Samale/Mazzuca/Phillips/Cohrs) have buggered up the end of the finale by over-ratiocination. Sure, the Berlin Phil are the better outfit but not so much that they blow away their country-cousins altogether. The latter are punchier.<br /><br />All in all, this is a three stars job.

B007MJ9LA2|4.0|Like many of the other reviewers, I was somewhat disappointed on my first listen to \\"Shape Shifter\\". It paled in comparison to Santana albums from the 70s which I still listen to with frequency.<br /><br />However, with multiple listens, some in which I stopped doing other things and simply LISTENED, the album has grown on me. It still has that Santana \\"cry\\" and passion. I look forward to more listening to come.<br /><br />There is good music making on this album. The key to appreciating it is: 1) don't compare it to the past (at least while you are listening), and 2) actually listen to the entire album several times with an open mind and without distractions.<br /><br />What I love in this album is actually what I don't like about it (simply because there is less of it there).<br /><br />In the 70s, Santana's bands consisted of headstrong, individualistic players. You could hear it in their playing. Therefore, the songs \\"breathed\\". There was push and pull. Players listened to each other and responded in energy as well as in the trading of \\"licks\\".<br /><br />There is still some of this on the new album, but, unfortunately, less. First, I think it may be modern recording techniques, with use of click tracks, not recording live in the studio, doing the solos after the rhythm tracks etc. Secondly, maybe Santana is so big now that the musicians he hires do not dare play anything without direction from him. I do not know if the second is true, but it sounds as if that could be the case.<br /><br />The best tracks on this album are the ones in which you can tell there is not a click track. The tempos accelerate and slow down. The worst tracks, in my opinion, are those in which the pace sounds like a clock and the drum parts, for example, sound almost like canned backing tracks.<br /><br />The guitar sound on this album is rich, full and big. Problem is, it sounds more or less the same throughout the album. On the old albums, there was more diversity in the guitar tone. Again, I have no idea how the new album was recorded, but it sounds as if the guitar was plugged directly into a recording console and digitally processed. In the old days, you would have a player playing different guitars through different \\"live\\" amplifiers being recorded by different microphones in different placements, all the time picking up different acoustics. Also, since much of the playing was taking place live, not overdubbed, you would also get the blended acoustics of the band playing in the same acoustic space. The new album sounds too \\"perfect\\" acoustically, and thus not as diverse.<br /><br />OK. So fellow, old Santana fans... Get your comparisons out of the way. They are valid comparisons, but don't let them deafen you. Go and actually listen to this album. Listen to it all the way through more than once. Stop your internet surfing for a few songs at least and just listen without distraction. There is still tons of beauty in this album. Therefore, I give it four stars.

0393061310|4.0|In this Pulitzer Prize winning book, Diamond attempts to answer the question of why the Western Civilization rose to power instead of other civilizations. His answer is that they lucked out on geography and ecology. Western civilizations were better able to domesticate animals and plants due to the varieties that they had available, and were therefore able to develop larger farming societies, allowing for development of crafts and \\"scientific exploration.\\" Also, because they were in larger groups, they were exposed at low levels to many germs, and developed immunity. Because of these factors, they were able to harness the power of guns, germs, and steel in their pursuit of world power. This was a fascinating book, and definitely worth reading even after watching the documentary. It's just packed full of information, the writing is smooth and articulate, and the research is extensive.

B007MAETA8|5.0|Mayer proves himself to be master of pretty much any style to which he sets his hand.  Here, he takes on easy 60's influenced country folk/rock, and he does it as if he were born to that style.  His guitar picking is first rate, simple and direct, always masterful.  His voice is in fantastic form, warmer, huskier than ever, sounding wiser for the life that he has lived.  He is so gifted at the guitar that sometimes we forget what a gifted singer/interpreter he is, and this gift for melody is in the forefront on this album.  Standouts for me include \\"Shadow Days\\", with an almost Harrison-esque opening guitar line segueing gently into a piano backed, steel guitar fueled easy groove.  He sings with conviction and beauty, a plea for understanding of a difficult breakup.  \\"I'm a good man, with a good hart, had a tough time, had a rough start...but I finally learned to let it go...now I'm right here, and right now, and I'm open, knowing somehow that my shadow days are over now\\".  I hope that his shadow days are over; this guy feels real, this feels like a really heartfelt tune, and is an instant favorite for me.  I also love the title track, with it's singalong feel and dense but lovely harmonies--and a divine harmonica.  The album ends with a reprise of the song, and it gives the whole album a completed feel.  \\"Whiskey, Whiskey, Whiskey\\" offers more plaintive harmonica over a great piano figure/guitar harmony, and some inventive chord changes that somehow feel right throughout.  John Mayer has always been good at crafting full albums worth of music--not a lot of filler on most of his albums; this album is just the same, but even more so--there is a sense that this was crafted as a set, each song as a piece unto a whole vision.  I haven't heard a Mayer album that I don't end up enjoying, but in the end, I think that this one has become a real special part of his musical tapestry, and an album that I will return to often and with genuine pleasure.  A long time ago, he saidthat he wanted to be the kind of guy that his fans will follow over the years as he grows.  I smiled at the time, thinking that he wouldn't really last that long.  I was wrong, and I'm so glad that I was, because this young man has something to say, the skills to say it well, and the heart to make me care.  Unreservedly recommended.

B005LAIHIQ|2.0|Maybe someday Tim Burton will wander back from cuckooland and once more make movies that grip you beyond the pretty,pretty visuals. DARK SHADOWS starts out so promisingly, getting me so enthused, and then, as it went on, the awfulness seeped in and built and built. Here I am, tearing out of the movie theater, in a foul, condemning mood. My date's asking that I not walk so fast, but I can't get out of there fast enough.<br /><br />I've no concrete point of reference to compare this film adaptation to, although the original television cult classic had been around for decades, lurking as this creaky pop culture trivia. But I thought the film instantly assumes a vibrant gothic presence and unveils a captivating introductory segment. In short strokes, this opening passage chronicles the establishing of a Maine fishing village in the 18th century and the life and death - and then undeath - of Barnabas Collins, scion of the founding Collins family. Barnabas Collins, who fails to love a comely witch and is cursed with vampirism. The villagers turn on him. They bury him in the dirt.<br /><br />- Barnabas Collins: \\"Tell me, future dweller, what is the year?\\"<br />- Willie Loomis: \\"1972.\\"<br /><br />Jackie Earle Haley (always reliable) plays Willie Loomis, butler to the present-day Collins clan and the first person Barnabas doesn't bite upon his release from entombment. Two hundred years (or, to be exact, 196 years) after being put in the ground, Barnabas bursts out of his coffin, shatters the chains encircling it, finds himself in today's modern era, in 1972. He makes a beeline for his ancestral home, now reduced to a dusty and dilapidated estate.<br /><br />Barnabas encounters his distant descendants and worms his way into their ambivalent graces, passing himself off as a relative from overseas. Only Elizabeth (Michelle Pfeiffer), the stern matriarch, knows the truth of Barnabas. In the passing centuries, the family fortunes have plummeted. Barnabas vows to return the Collins family to its former stature of wealth and prominence. He doesn't know yet that he's set himself against a familiar supernatural presence. It's sort of apropos that in 1972 the Eagles released their single \\"Witchy Woman.\\"<br /><br />You eyeball this glittering lineup and assume that you're in for some powerhouse performances. Except that this unfolds mostly as a one man show. Johnny Depp is undeniably fabulous, and if that's all you're looking for in this flick, then you'll walk away sated. You have to allow for Depp's affectations, his character's arch mannerisms, those expressive, elongated fingers of his. You have to, I guess, allow for his character's lack of accountability. Barnabas pretty much gets away with murdering a bunch of people, but never mind, never mind. I frankly love what Depp does with this role. But then most everyone else follows suit, and that negates the effectiveness of what Depp was trying to do. Pfeiffer, Helena Bonham Carter, even Chlo Grace Moretz all submit such stylized performances that I had a problem relating to them. Only Eva Green, as the wicked and long-lived Angelique Bouchard, brings it with vivid energy and great enthusiasm. Down the centuries, the witch Angelique had become a powerufl and influential figure in this fishing community. Compelled by dark designs, she promptly renews acquaintances with the lover who once spurned her.<br /><br />I admit to relishing the fish-out-of-water gags surrounding Barnabas' 18th century sensibilities colliding with the modern-day culture of 1972. He gazes, entranced, at a lava lamp, thinking it composed of blood and urine. He hangs out with hippies. He thinks the \\"M\\" on a MacDonald's stands for \\"Mephistopholes.\\" But the best bit may be Alice Cooper's cameo at the ball, with Barnabas gazing at him thru opera glasses and muttering: \\"Ugliest woman I have ever seen.\\" It's a shame the film denies us the thrill of a unique interaction between Barnabas and Alice. It's just one more missed opportunity.<br /><br />I don't think Tim Burton can craft an ugly-looking film if his life depended on it. DARK SHADOWS is cinematically well-executed. It treats us to a series of sumptuous visuals. But it's hollow gratification, yo. The story suffers from poor character development, the lack of a narrative drive, the actors' languid approach, and an underdeveloped and absolutely unearned love story. About the last, Barnabas keeps refering to the slim governess he adores as possessing these \\"fertile birthing hips,\\" except that she totally doesn't, so what was he going on about? The campiness and sense of satire are so marked that they pretty much overwhelm whatever meager grounding the film has. I admire Depp's performance, but I don't care about his character. I don't care about any of these characters. I don't perceive them as anything but caricatures. Anyway, the narrative, ultimately, takes a back seat to Depp's doing his weird sh--. To cap off the awfulness of it all, things fall apart at the climax. Somewhere along this final sequence, the film even finds the nerve to drop a reveal about Chlo Grace Moretz's character that is jarring and comes out of left field. In the past few years Burton and Depp's collaboration has become a gimmicky thing. It's disconcerting that SLEEPY HOLLOW - the last film by these two that I've really enjoyed - is thirteen years old. I had such high hopes for this one. I love several things about this DARK SHADOWS. But I dislike so much more about it.unity.<br /><br />I don't think Tim Burton can craft an ugly-looking film if his life depended on it. DARK SHADOWS is cinematically well-executed. 0143117564|4.0|While some have said this was a simple sit down, dive in, and finish easily book, I would have to disagree. Inherent Vice for readers born after the 60s offers a tricky plotline to follow. Thankfully we have a magnificent leader in Pynchon to guide us through this psychedelic time warp. Like Magellan navigating the tricky straights of the southern point of South America, Pynchon navigates through all of the major conflicts of the 60s, and does so with ease through his character of Doc Sportello.<br />Doc Sportello is a Private Investigator, and public genius. To the police on payroll, he is classified as nothing more than a hippie vagrant like everyone else living in the Venice Beach area of Hollywood where his practice is located. But to the hippies, he is a regular Sherlock Holmes. While this doesn't hit on specifics like the Kent State Massacre, the difference between peace loving civilians and the police department is clearly shown. This key difference offers en extreme historical significance, adding to the underlying theme of the book.<br />Doc is introduced going about his daily business when he is interrupted by an ex-girlfriend, Shasta. As Shasta and he reconnect he discovers her new boyfriend, a billionaire construction mogul, will go missing and Doc is asked to help. A construction mogul having a billion dollars seems a bit outrageous, especially for the 60's, but Pynchon does everything for a reason. The loss of power and money in LA, and the introduction of corruption and deceit, represents as a whole, a new idea of how things are working in America in the 60's. As Doc attempts to discover his whereabouts hilarity ensues. Pynchon's trick to unraveling the 60's without becoming too political or factual is to use comedy to mask the deeper meaning. Doc is a peace loving hippy trying to find a straight edge, money loving mogul. This ying-yang difference is what creates the humor as Doc battles guns with weed, and uses love as a tactic to gather evidence.<br/>The genius behind Doc, excluding Pynchon, is his near addiction to smoking weed. His marijuana usage helps bring to life the psychosis of the 60s in the writing and plot line. He's not simply corrupting his lungs when he indulges on Vietnamese weed; rather, Pynchon is stating something of much bigger historical significance. But to Doc, everything is just a haze. The ambiguity of the case presents a parallel to Doc's hazy thoughts. He is able to float the line between a serious detective and a laughable hippie all thanks to his favorite herb. Overall though, it is Pynchon's ability to float the line between the serious issues of the 1960's and the hilarity of a story with a character that rivals the pink panther, that makes this book truly a great read.

Although I was familiar with the inhumane and brutal treatment of North Korean prisoners, Shin's autobiography put these atrocities in a new light.  North Koreans have been brainwashed for over 50 years, made to believe anyone the state deems an enemy (even a child) is worthy of the most brutal punishment.  Shin's story reflects this taken to the most severe degree: children born into an environment with no morality, no humanity, and no love.<br /><br />The story moves fast, taking the reader from Shin's earliest memories, through his trials in the camp as a youth, and his escape from the prison and on to South Korea. Shin knew nothing beyond the hell of the camp, the torture, pain.  He had nothing to miss from the outside.  What would happen if that knowledge invaded his space? James used that word)<br /><br />James knows so little about modern-day sexual experience among college-age students that she wants us to believe that Ana has never touched herself, let alone touched herself with the goal of achieving orgasm. EVERY mentally-healthy young woman cannot wait to find out what an orgasm feels like. A longtime best friend like Kate, who is portrayed as persistently bossy, would have never let Ana graduate and enter the \\"real world\\" without having had an orgasm. If all verbal persistence had failed, Kate would have purchased a dildo for Ana with one or two words. I'd choose \\"gentle\\" and \\"explore\\" -- and not let up until Ana had turned that dildo on.<br /><br />Besides, I cannot believe Ana has not experienced a female erection while reading fiction. My god, even in the tiny rural town I grew up in, a single copy of Terry Southern's \\"Candy\\" made its way to every girl in my class and even to me, surprising because I wasn't an \\"in\\" girl. Its effect on me was as physical as it was mental. And its scenes gave me enough fantasy material to keep me entertained for years.<br /><br />Where were \\"Tom Jones\\" and \\"Moll Flanders\\" in Ana's college education? Where was Chaucer? And don't tell me that she never read \\"Lolita,\\" Nabokov's triumph of eroticism, desire, obsession, and complete mastery -- astonishing for a non-native American and non-native speaker/writer of English -- of a factually and linguistically perfect description of 1939 America and Americans.<br /><br />If James read \\"Lolita,\\" she'd have figured out that Nabokov traveled in an automobile everywhere Humbert Humbert went, painstakingly detailing what he saw and heard. Nabokov never had to ask his readers to suspend disbelief. He transported every American reader to a world they knew instinctively as their very own. Every sentient reader effortlessly believed what Nabokov wrote about his characters and their surroundings.<br /><br />Why does James not realize that Ana's supposed innocence is in fact psychologically and physically abnormal? That no mentally healthy Ana could be separated from her body without a physical abnormality or psychological trauma severe enough to disconnect her from every whole young woman's natural desire and sexual reflexes that are impossible to suppress?  Why does the author presume she can get away with having us believe that no mentally healthy Ana ever found any male student, T.A. (teaching assistant), and/or professor so desirable that she effortlessly had fantasies about them and let them near her?<br /><br />How can James make us believe that no male student, T.A. and/or professor was overwhelmed by her physical appearance, her confidence, her sense of humor, and her smarts that he could not help but throw himself at her which, if she were psychologically normal, she would find immensely pleasurable and sexually responsive (to varying degrees)?<br /><br />And how about those customers at the hardware store? Men who work construction are sometimes unusually hot -- that's why the male lover in many great romance novels (Lady Chatterly's Lover, anyone?) have manual jobs.<br /><br />Besides construction workers sure to visit that hardware store regularly, the larger area is populated by commercial fishermen, loggers, professional divers, U.S. Coast Guard search & rescue teams who risk (and lose) their lives in the *dangerous* waters of the Columbia River and the Pacific Ocean. Then there are Native Americans descendants of nearby tiny but independent tribes whose men raided each other's tribes, stealing and enslaving women. Those eyes, those cheekbones. Their feline, silent moves. Their need to go back in time, perhaps even conquering others' women.<br /><br />The crass displays of capitalism are amusing, from a British writer who has no clue that, in the greater Seattle area, those with real money NEVER display their wealth. As the son of two wealthy, socially prominent parents and surrounded by the wealthy parents of classmates, Christian would know (if James knew) that only the nouveau riche would drive a flashy sports car. I've seen Bill Gates in many settings, and never once did he indicate in any way that he had a penny but for the location of his seat at Sonics basketball games. Even his car was purposely a typical mid-size beige sedan, and he drove himself everywhere.<br /><br />Furthermore, Bill Gates would have never given his girlfriend (later his wife) an automobile that screamed \\"MONEY!!!\\"  Why not? 0061692042|5.0|\\"First, I'll tell about the robbery our parents committed. Then about the murders, which happened later.\\" That's the first two lines of the book.<br /><br />Beyond the vast ocean of Saskatchewanian wheat fields, burrowed with the detritus of past lives and half-lives, a fifteen-year-old boy is marooned on a forgotten prairie land with fugitives and transients, like a scrap of driftwood or a windblown, bone-cracked bottle. His surname is a mystery for twelve chapters; it's released, finally, like a swift, soft teardrop.<br /><br />Ford's great American/Canadian novel is a coming-of-age adventure tale about realizing one's own identity through narrative, memories, and self-examination. Moreover, it's about crossing, dissolving, and abnegating boundaries, physically as well as psychically, and generating rapport between our internal selves and the external world. At the heart of this story are the borders we cross and the crosses we bear. Symbolic, too, is that Saskatchewan is the only Canadian province with no geographic physical properties to denote boundaries. The abandoned young Montanan hero redefines divisions and indivisible spaces with deep reflection.<br /><br />\\"If anything, the similarity to America made its foreignness profound...\\"<br /><br />This half of a twin, living as if he were an orphan, tells us his story with tender wit and optimism amidst the garbage heap of objects and dwellings inhabited by outlaws and goose hunters. He was taught to \\"always know something that I could relinquish.\\" And he was able to see the world as its opposite, and draw strength from that.<br /><br />The view of melting pot America ultimately merges with the cultural mosaic of Canada, and becomes a theme. In lesser hands, many aspects of this book could have seemed repetitive, tautological, but Ford amplifies the meaning of every revolving concept by mining it to its irreducible essence. Nothing is diminished in this masterpiece. The themes are potent, and not diluted with hollow slogans.<br /><br />The story's hero narrates dramatic, life-changing events that happened to him and his twin sister, Berner, after their parents robbed a North Dakota bank in 1960. The twins' father, Beverly Parsons, was an Air Force bombardier from Alabama, a smiling, talkative, self-serving handsome six-footer who returned from the war ultimately misunderstanding the world he came home to, and unsuited to the woman he fell in love with. But he embraced all that America stood for.<br /><br />Mother, Neeva, was a bespectacled intellectual from Tacoma, the daughter of educated Jewish immigrants, a woman who didn't want to assimilate with the people and land of Montana. The mismatch of parents created a terrible, unresolved tension that was chronicled in Neeva's journal and left as part of a legacy of loneliness for the children to untangle or inherit.<br /><br />This nerve-shattering story is filled with vivid incidents and characters alike, propelled by charm and clarity, provocative as it is diverting. Short, fluent chapters maintain a lyrical, vibrating rhythm. It is accessible, engaging, eloquently woven and plotted, not one word out of place, not one event unnecessary. The prose is unprepossessing and yet noble, austere but lush, stark yet playful, elegiac but bright, polished with all the messiness of life.<br /><br />It moves with the alacrity of a gazelle, spins together with effortless grace. As radiant and moving as a cinnamon sun and as sublime as a silver moon. This is a sensuous departure from the Frank Bascombe novels. The understated narrator's voice is flawlessly vulnerable, wry, and lightly brushed with a mournful surrender.<br /><br />As an addendum, I read that Ford is planning to write more novels set in Canada. According to Harper Collins, \\"We are thrilled to be publishing the first of Ford's novels to be set in this country\\" (north of the 49th parallel).llow slogans.<br /><br />The story's hero narrates dramatic, life-changing events that happened to him and his twin sister, Berner, after their parents robbed a North Dakota bank in 1960. The culture around us seems to embrace parenting that caters to the child in (what seems to us) unhealthy ways.  In contrast, Druckerman's observations point toward a parenting style that is both warm and authoritative, lenient yet structured.  She doesn't necessarily give pointers on how to achieve this careful balance; rather, she simply notes that it produces fairly well-behaved children who thrive within the structure provided to them.  What I really liked about this book was that Druckerman is a real parent; that is, she, like many parents, doubts herself and points out her failures (but she isn't too hard on herself or self-depreciating).  Reading her observations, the reader gets the impression that she is learning as much as the reader as she goes along.  Finally, I appreciated that all of her observations are precluded by caveats (not ALL French children behave a certain way much like not ALL American children behave a certain way).  Overall, this was an enjoyable, take the advice/leave the advice read.
0307946711|4.0|I was all prepared to gush about this book. How it's not like the other books; not Nicholas Sparks or Jodi Picoult or any of that nonsense. It's a great idea, and a great story, with a great writing style. Dexter and Emma become friends on the day after their college graduation, July 15, 1988, and they have strong romantic feelings for each other, but the situation isn't right for them to get together. (As is so often true in life). But, they manage to stay friends. Through years and years and jobs and emotions and relationships and breakups and gains and losses. It's not easy, and they are both far from perfect, and they fail (a lot), but they manage. And that's the most beautiful thing I can think of. Dex and Em, Em and Dex. Best friends, estranged friends, bickering acquaintances, completely in love but never talk about it because the situation isn't right. I won't lie and tell you it hasn't made me want to call my own \\"Dexter\\", and tell him I forgive him for not calling me on my birthday two years ago, and I'm sorry I told him to f*** off the last time he drunk-dialed me. Because neither of us is perfect, but if someone wrote a book about one day of my life every year, I'd want him to be in some of them.<br /><br />The format of the book is magical. There's one chapter per year, the events of July 15, every year. And nothing in between. The story moves a long at a faster pace that way, because you're eager to find out not just what happens that day, but also what has happened in the past 364 days. Sometimes the chapters end with a little teaser, since the reader will never directly know what happens the very next day. But the answer is easily deduced from the next year's chapter. It even has repetition (which I love when done correctly) to emphasize the ways in which their lives are the same for multiple years, and the ways in which they come full circle.<br /><br />And then I got to page 348, at which point the book became complete crap. And stayed complete crap through the end. Why can't authors understand that a book doesn't have to end in both romance and horrible, shocking tragedy? The contrived scenarios are unrealistic, and if I'm going to read a story that is unrealistic why would I read one that doesn't make me happy? (And doesn't have dragons in it?) Life, love, relationships, and friendships almost always, like the world, end not with a bang but with a whimper. This novel had the potential to reflect that, but fell short.<br /><br />This is a book about growing-up, and the difficulties of friendship, and the difficulties of ordinary life (not contrived tragic life) in general. I recommend it to anyone who wishes that The \\"Unbearable Lightness of Being\\" didn't suck so bad.<br /><br />But for the love of all that is holy, STOP READING AT THE BOTTOM OF PAGE 347.<br /><br />Please email me if you would like my address to send me your copy of the book, so I can glue pages 348-435 together and return it to you.<br /><br />I'm not joking.<br /><br />The End.

0307352145|5.0|The extensive research behind this book brings out some interesting points. American society is generally more extroverted than Asian countries, and our preference for the gregarious person over the serious thinker has spilled over into our school classrooms. The typical classroom with its emphasis on \\"group learning\\" and extensive class participation is not a friendly place for an introverted child. This is particularly disturbing when you consider that the tendency for introversion has a definite genetic basis, and attempts to train children to fit in may be extremely stressful to them. There is no specific advice here, simply a wake-up call for parents; monitor the school environment to be sure appropriate learning and socialization strategies are applied to your children.<br /><br />The chapter on Cain's experience at a cultish Tony Robbins pep rally is worth the price of this book. The emphasis on schmoozing and positive thinking in overwhelmingly extroverted Harvard Business School students and in the business world is a symptom of a serious systemic defect in our society. Some victims of this mentality are unemployed people who are sold expensive Robbins-style classes in hopes of landing a job by projecting confidence. (Barbara Ehrenreich develops this theme in her book \\"Bright-Sided: How positive thinking is undermining America\\".) The \\"team building\\" fad in the workplace has not resulted in more productive employees.<br /><br />Corporations typically overlook their quiet talented employees for upper management positions in favor of aggressive self-promoters, with unfortunate results. Some of the research findings are truly shocking, and Cain has missed an opportunity to develop them further. For example, it has been shown that extraverts take more risks than introverts. Combine that with the fact that 20% of the top three executives at Fortune 500 companies are Harvard Business School graduates, and we can see this as a factor in the 2008 financial crash. Extreme risk taking was a major cause, yet these are some of the same executives who amassed multimillion dollar increases in compensation since the economy tanked.  Is there a correlation between identified corporate looters and whether  they are predominantly extraverts, or not?

I really enjoy the guitar/drum breakdown at the chorus. Not sure why this was the first UK single and America got the far less interesting \\"Blood For Poppies\\". Then again, all these record companies are reaching senility and making less sense, so...I guess that explains it? Either way: great song.<br /><br />Man On A Wire - Kind of sounds like Peaches. This brings up an interesting aspect of Garbage's music. More often than not, it isn't good Garbage songs versus bad Garbage songs, it's super catchy and well written radio playable Garbage songs versus skippable Garbage songs. I can't really think of an out and out bad Garbage song, just a bunch that I'd rather not listen to in favor of a different, better Garbage song. The delineation is clearest, I feel, on their second album Version 2.0. There are two types of songs on that album: the radio singles and those that are not radio singles. The former just have that perfect mix of hooks and melody and everything else that makes a song great and super replayable, and the latter just feels a bit...wrong. It isn't really something I can point out, just this abstract feeling of \\"these are better than those\\". All this to say: \\"Man On A Wire\\" is skippable. For me at least.<br /><br />Beloved Freak - A disclaimer: I'm a huge Eels fan, and, as the title reminds me of \\"Beautiful Freak\\", the two songs will always be compared in my mind and the Garbage song will always be found lacking. Sorry guys. Also, I don't really like the song a whole lot. It's too smaltzy and saccharine. \\"You're not alone, you're great, you're special, let your light shine...\\". It all just sounds like a grade school teacher consoling a kid that all the other kids are calling a weirdo because he like Magic cards instead of baseball cards or plays D&D or wears a cape or something. Honestly, I blame my own cynicism. It's a sweet, warm, encouraging song and my sensibilities just don't lie that way...even though I, too, own a cape.<br /><br />Overall, this album shows that Garbage is still capable of evolution. Maybe not lyrically so much, but sonically. Most of the music on this album has so much going on under the drums, bass, guitar and vocals that it makes listening with headphones and listening on speakers two very different experiences, both rich and highly enjoyable.<br />I'm very interested in hearing the other eleven or so songs the band wrote and recorded for the album, as they're known for having some really excellent b-sides out there, many of them better than some album cuts, in my opinion. I don't think I'll consider Not Your Kind Of People truly complete until the other half has been released and can be fit in and around this first half. In any case, Garbage is certainly back and, hopefully, able to step right back into where they were almost ten years ago.<br />Oh, and also, Butch Vig is awesome.

B007MAETA8|5.0|In his excellent DVD \\"Where the Light Is\\" filmed in 2007, John Mayer adamantly insists that those who think they know his music are mistaken. He suggests that a true artist should be a musical chameleon capable of changing the face he shows the world. Mayer also suggests that even he doesn't know what that true face may be. Nothing he has released since then contradicts that viewpoint. This superb record, which hones his artistry to bare essentials in both song writing and production, is merely the latest evidence of that. Sounding as if it might have been recorded in his basement, Born and Raised features the very essence of Mayer's artistry. The songs are melodic and memorable without being cloying. Mayer's lyrics are probing and insightful but never self indulgent. With a decidedly Country tinge that is nevertheless strangely timeless in its appeal, this is Mayer's least commercial and most accomplished CD to date. Songs like \\"Speak For Me\\" and \\"Born and Raised\\" sound as if they have always existed and that Mayer simply grasped them out of the air, put them in his guitar case and took them home. He has traveled a long way from his beginnings as a pop heart throb. Mayer's talents as guitarist, singer and song writer make him today's most versatile musician, each new release a snapshot of where he currently resides. This latest release showcases John Mayer living at home, just south of the crossroads where American music has its roots in both folk and the blues. Born and Raised is a strong release from a gifted artist.<br /><br />Mike Birman

B005G0ETV0|5.0|I have always loved Rubinstein. I might even venture to say that, though he may not have produced the definitive recordings of all pieces within his repertoire, to my ears he never produced anything that sounded anything less than beautiful. Here is a man whose delight in the sound- and soul-world of music ripples forth in every arpeggio, whose identification with the message behind the music emerges from behind the dying into silence of every lingering fermata.<br /><br />There are individual discs here that are simply mind-blowing, from the early Chopin to the Beethoven concerti with Leinsdorf to the Mozart concerti with Wallenstein and Krips to the collaborations with Heifetz, Szeryng, Piatigorsky, and the Guarneri Quartet. Indeed, as much as I love the concerto works, Rubinstein brings something so inimitable to chamber music, some deep commitment to ensemble work that is all too often missing from the later generations of virtuosi.<br /><br />In short, let me accord this release my heartiest recommendation. It was worth every penny, and every hour of listening has been glorious. And I haven't even gotten to the DVDs yet!

0307352145|4.0|'Quiet' is a timely series of well-researched essays converted into a book.The footnotes lead to impressive reading material for anyone interested in a wide scope of areas associated introversion. Many of the comments and concerns raised by other readers will be easily answered by the footnotes at the end of the book. I am an INTJ and I can sense some of the challenges Susan seems to have faced in writing this book - There is tons of meta-analyses, followed by some prescriptive advise, followed by some anecdotes about famous introverts. The fundamental mental premise of this book is that the world needs to improve how it deals with introverts and deaddict itself from the culture of extraversion, a valid concern in the complex dynamic world we see today. The content is valuable; initial chapters successfully explain how the finer aspects of how to differentiate between introversion and some traits that unfairly get generalized/attributed to introverts.The problem is that the book tends to make a diffused case as it tackles multiple facets and lacks some editorial insight. With so much to say, the book falls into the trap of trying to make every case that it can and ends up with breadth and depth alternating unpredictably. It makes me wonder if the 'extroverts' of the world will be able to complete this book.

1400032717|3.0|I read this at the recommendation of a friend and am now left in an awkward position wherein I have to return the book underwhelmed.  She was so insistent that I read it, too.  Bummer.<br /><br />It's not that the book is bad.  It started off with an addictive momentum and when I was away from it, I found myself missing it.  I was enthusiastic about the read when it was in my hands because it was different and unpretentious.  Briefly, a dog is murdered and the crime needs solving.  The protagonist, Christopher, logs his investigation in a sharp, first-person narrative.  He reads as a troubled child, likely affected with something akin to Aspergers or a autism-like mental illness.  Emotionally void, he traverses the incident like a robot filling in dry spots in the narrative with math problems and notations about the manic mannerisms he uses to keep himself occupied and scream-free.  It was all interesting, occasionally sad, and I adhered to it willingly because the story is a mystery and there are some twists and turns initially that were making it worth my time.<br /><br />But then it flattened out and stayed that way through its ending.  I hate that I'm complaining about this because the story was realistically grounded and not at all too fantastic to believe, which is something I typically lean on.  But, man, I eventually became bored with the whole thing and found myself reading on autopilot while daydreaming about other things.  (This may sound ridiculous and irrational, but this is the sort of story that I would have gobbled up ravenously had it been non-fiction.  A true story written by its little life astronaut who opted to track for us the situation given here?  I'd have been all over that.)<br /><br />And I understand what it was the author was trying to gift to us but for me it just wasn't enough.  I commend him for giving a voice to this tortured teen but I needed something more to feel like I'd experienced something remarkable.  Still, the read was decent enough that if I see Mark Haddon's name on any future works, I'll consider handing him my hard-earned money because I do believe he's a gifted writer and capable of producing stories of substance.  This one, however, just didn't do it for me.<br /><br />I thank you for your time.<br /><br />- tnt enough that if I see Mark Haddon's name on any future works, I'll consider handing him my hard-earned money because I do believe he's a gifted writer and capable of producing stories of substance. 0545265355|5.0|The Hunger Games Trilogy is simply a great read.  It will pull you away into literally, a whole other world with characters well-developed enough to feel like friends after the first book, let alone the third book.  The author is very creative in her world-building while keeping concepts simple enough for the novel to remain a youth novel.  While the books have been talked about for their romantic aspects (which are extraordinarily well done), this is merely one strong thread in a plot complex with politics, deception, family, and the skills (often unanticipated) that one needs to survive.<br /><br />If you are a parent considering whether or not this book would be appropriate for your child, do be aware of the violence in the books (not horribly graphic, but pervasive in all 3 books).  No worries about language or sex.  If you let your child watch PG-13 movies, then they are ready for \\"The Hunger Games.\\"<br /><br />For a quick plot summary - imagine the world as we know it - gone.  A war of massive proportions has dissolved all current governments, and all that is left are 13 \\"districts\\" headed by one government: Panem.  To exert its control over the districts, the government destroys district 13 completely and then forces the other 12 districts to offer up 2 children each year and enter them in the Hunger Games - a fight to the death with only 1 winner.  The first novel follows Katniss and Peta as they are chosen to compete in the games.  While the fight for life inside the arena rages,the first sparks of a fight for freedom from the corrupt government begin to grow on the outside.  The second novel (MY FAVORITE OF THE THREE) finds another year passed and another Hunger Games on the horizon.  This time, however, past Hunger Game WINNERS are thrown back into the arena for an incredibly creative game fought by the best survivors from several generations.  Their fight to come out alive a second time turns into a fight to change the world they return to.  Which leaves us with the third novel - a book about a rebellion long overdue, led by Panem's surviving greatest competitors.  The Hunger Games tributes bring the games to the very capitol itself where once again, there can only be one survivor - the government or the people.<br /><br />This summary does not even hint at the wonderful language, characters, and incredible creativity of the author.  The beauty of these books is definitely in their execution.  As some characters are lost, new (and just as wonderful) characters are introduced.  The villains are incredibly complex for Youth Fiction.  The author beautifully shows characters as they walk the line between winning and winning at all costs - which might turn someone into the very thing they are fighting against.  Again, the world building is amazing (the details of the games are so much fun, as are the descriptions of the districts and the capitol and its people).<br /><br />My ONLY complaint is in the third novel.  While I certainly am glad that the author did not focus too heavily on the love-triangle while the entire Hunger Games world is at war, the way she handles its resolution is lacking.  So many other plot threads are wonderfully tied up and resolved - to leave the love story where she did really creates a serious imperfection.  It wouldn't have taken much - one or two scenes added between the characters (and one real breakout scene between Katniss and the one she does eventually fall in love with) - and this thread too could have given the incredible satisfaction that the rest of the book gives.  It's really so bad - I am convinced they will change the end for the movie versions!  No director is going to direct that...<br /><br />Still - this series ABSOLUTELY is worth the hype and all the praise it has received.  Collins steps into some mighty big shoes (those of J.K. Rowling and Stephanie Meyers) to make reading a true pleasure.<br /><br />Carrie Salo<br />Author of the Supernatural Thriller - The Soundingus with the third novel - a book about a rebellion long overdue, led by Panem's surviving greatest competitors. The album also featured two songs by Alter Bridge frontman Myles Kennedy who would also go on to front Slash's group for his world tour during which he would sing songs from Slash's former bands as well as tracks from the album, so every night Kennedy would be filling the shoes of Axl Rose, Scott Weiland, Andrew Stockdale, Ian Astbury and Chris Cornell it was a job that he performed admiringly as his voice handled all the different ranges. So for Slash's second album Kennedy performs all the tracks, it seems like a natural progression and a working relationship has been built up by the two men.<br /><br />The album itself \\"Apocalyptic Love\\" is a rock fuelled funk filled adrenaline rush of old school rock and roll and it's clear from the outset that the album has one intention and that's to rock, it's an album full of great riffs such as the opening song the title track \\"Apocalyptic Love\\" with its funk driven riff that has Myles Kennedy using his full vocal range to drive the song on and features a great sing along chorus. \\"One Last Thrill\\" starts out with a scream in the background before Kennedy delivers the lyrics at 100 miles rate; it's a great piece of punk rock. It shows Kennedy has many different styles of vocals. It's also topped off by a trademark Slash solo. There's some great drumming and bass playing from Brent Fitz and Todd kerns on the song \\"Standing In The Sun\\", both Fitz and Kerns are part of slash's live band.<br /><br />The song \\"Anastasia\\" starts out with a gentle flamenco style acoustic guitar (think end of double talkin' jive) before kicking into a \\"Sweet Child O' Mine\\" riff and changes again and features a great solo from slash as well as Kennedy going from low to high with his vocal. \\"Bad Rain\\" has a pure rock swagger about it with its pounding drums and a grooving riff. The first single \\"You're A Lie\\" is a good indication of the album with its killer chorus and sleek guitar playing, it shows how much the Slash and Myles Kennedy partnership has grown.<br /><br />Slash seems to have found his perfect frontman foil in Myles Kennedy and \\"Apocalyptic Love\\" is an unashamedly full blown rock album, one that owes much to Slash's 70's rock influences. It will be interesting to see where they go from here as the they continue to gel even more, Slash has stated in recent interviews that making the album was the most fun he's had in a long time and listening to the album its clear to hear the chemistry that they have.

B007H9B6KA|4.0|It's been seven years since Garbage released their last studio album 2005's \\"Bleed Like Me\\", in that same year the band ended a world tour abruptly that saw the band cancel the end of their tour and go on an indefinite hiatus, it seemed the band had reached the end of the road as they were sick of record label interference, life on the road and sick of the sight of each other, after all this is a band that always had a fiery relationship with each other that had seen many a studio bust up. So as lead singer Shirley Manson went off to make a solo album (which has never been released) as well as star in the TV series Terminator: The Sarah Connor Chronicles and drummer Butch Vig went back to producing albums it seemed unlikely Garbage would end their hiatus. Thankfully that all changed as the band regrouped and confirmed work had begun on a new album that would be released on their own STUNVOLUME label, it seemed that working without a major label suited the band and that they were all getting on and (whisper it quietly) having fun working together again. So finally we have studio album number five \\"Not Your Kind Of People\\" an album which features all the hallmarks of Garbage's signature sound of a rock/pop fusion.<br /><br />The album starts off with \\"Automatic Systematic Habit\\" which is a disco filled rock song that has Manson saying she won't be anybody's \\"Dirty Little Secret\\", the song has a big pounding beat and some distorted vocals; it's a great way to kick off the album. \\"Big Bright World\\" is reminiscent of the Version 2.0 era with its slow build up before exploding into life. The first track we got to hear from the album was \\"Blood for Poppies\\" which at the time seemed a strange choice as the lead single but it's a song that will grow on the listener over time and features a trippy breakdown towards the end. One of the standout tracks is \\"control\\" with its intelligent use of a harmonica and big beats with a chopped up vocal effect and a great lyric in \\"It's always darkest before the dawn\\".<br /><br />The title track \\"Not Your Kind of People\\" which is a slow paced song which has a dreamy 70's sound with a guitar at time not sounding unlike Lynard Skynards \\"Freebird\\". If there is one thing Garbage did better than anybody it is on big sounding numbers such as \\"Vow\\" from their debut album and with the song \\"Battle In Me\\" that is what Garbage do, it's a big sounding track with an aggressive vocal from Manson and a big heavy guitar/drum chorus, it's a song made for playing live. \\"Man On A Wire\\" is another stomper that's played at 100 miles per hour. Former Jane's Addiction bassist Eric Avery (who will play bass on the bands tour) plays bass on both \\"Battle In Me\\" and \\"Man On A Wire\\". \\"I Hate Love\\" is another dance rock track that's full of effects.<br /><br />It's good to have Garbage back with album number 5 which is a strong return for the band and shows there's still plenty of life left in them yet and it seems the time apart has revitalised them and got them on the same page musically again. There's plenty on here for long-time fans of the band and hopefully convert some new fans.

There is a finish to the initial story line after the murders but it's common in many books to spend time with a story at the beginning and end of the book with another the 'center' of it so that didn't bother me and I found it interesting as, if nothing else, we learn more about who our main characters are.
